LESSCLOSE=/usr/bin/lesspipe %s %s
CONDA_DEFAULT_ENV=base
PYDEVD_IPYTHON_COMPATIBLE_DEBUGGING=1
GPG_AGENT_INFO=/run/user/1000/gnupg/S.gpg-agent:0:1
GJS_DEBUG_OUTPUT=stderr
QT_IM_MODULE=ibus
GIT_PAGER=cat
PWD=/home/wallabot/Documentos/web
CLICOLOR=1
XDG_DATA_DIRS=/usr/share/ubuntu:/usr/local/share/:/usr/share/:/var/lib/snapd/desktop
XDG_CONFIG_DIRS=/etc/xdg/xdg-ubuntu:/etc/xdg
VSCODE_CODE_CACHE_PATH=/home/wallabot/.config/Code/CachedData/6261075646f055b99068d3688932416f2346dd3b
CONDA_EXE=/home/wallabot/anaconda3/bin/conda
CONDA_PREFIX=/home/wallabot/anaconda3
VSCODE_PID=3897

							

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
View an environment variable with the command echo.

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
To view a specific environment variable we can do it by using the command echo followed by the symbol $ and the variable name

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!echo $HOME

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
/home/wallabot

							

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Modifying an environment variable for a terminal session

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
We can modify an environment variable for the active terminal session, for example, let's add a new path to the PATH variable. First we see what is in it

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!echo $PATH

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
/home/wallabot/anaconda3/bin:/home/wallabot/anaconda3/condabin:/usr/local/sbin:/usr/local/bin:/usr/sbin:/usr/bin:/sbin:/bin:/usr/games:/usr/local/games:/snap/bin

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
Now we add a new address

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!PATH=$PATH:"subdirectorio

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
Let's go back to see what is inside PATH.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!echo $PATH

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
/home/wallabot/anaconda3/bin:/home/wallabot/anaconda3/condabin:/usr/local/sbin:/usr/local/bin:/usr/sbin:/usr/bin:/sbin:/bin:/usr/games:/usr/local/games:/snap/bin:subdirectorio

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
We see that the subdirectory directory has been added.

						

					

				

			

	
			

				

					

						

							
The problem with this method is that when we open a new terminal this change in PATH will not be maintained.

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Modifying an environment variable for all terminal sessions

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
We go to the home folder

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
terminal("cd /home/wallabot")

								

							

						

					

				

			

				

					

						

							
Here in the home we list all files with the flag -a (all)

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!ls -a

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
.		.eclipse		       .pki
..		Escritorio		       Plantillas
.afirma		.gitconfig		       .platformio
anaconda3	.gnupg			       .profile
.audacity-data	Imágenes		       .psensor
.bash_history	.ipython		       Público
.bash_logout	.java			       .python_history
.bashrc		.jupyter		       snap
.cache		.lesshst		       .ssh
.conda		Lightworks		       .sudo_as_admin_successful
.config		.Lightworks.thereCanBeOnlyOne  .thunderbird
.cortex-debug	.local			       Vídeos
.cyberghost	logiops			       .vnc
.dbus		.MCTranscodingSDK	       .vscode
Descargas	.mozilla		       .wget-hsts
.docker		Música
Documentos	.nv

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
We see that there is a file named .bashrc, this file is the file that has the configuration of our bash

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
terminal("cat .bashrc", max_lines_output=3)

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
# ~/.bashrc: executed by bash(1) for non-login shells.
# see /usr/share/doc/bash/examples/startup-files (in the package bash-doc)
# for examples
	 ...
fi
unset __conda_setup
# <<< conda initialize <<<

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
This file is the one that configures the terminal each time a new terminal is opened, so if we edit the PATH variable in it, this change will be maintained for all the new terminal windows we open.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
To modify the PATH variable inside the configuration file we have to add the following line to the file

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
`

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
PATH=$PATH: "subdirectory"

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
`

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Create aliases for all sessions

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
We already saw how to create command aliases, but it also happened that they were lost every time we closed a terminal session, so that this does not happen, we also add them to the configuration file .bashrc. For example, in my case I have added the following lines

						

					

				

			

	
			

				

					

						

							
alias ll='ls -l'
alias la='ls -a'
alias lh='ls -h'
alias lha='ls -lha'

						

					

				

			

	
	
	
	
	
			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Search commands

								
							

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Search for binaries with which.

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
The first search command we are going to see is which which allows us to find the path to the binaries.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!which python

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
/home/wallabot/anaconda3/bin/python

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
However, if we look for something that is not in one of the PATH paths, which will not be able to tell us the path.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!which cd

								

							

						

					

				

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
File search with find

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
To search for a file with find we have to indicate from which path we want to find the file, followed by the -name flag and the name of the file we want to search for

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!find ~ -name "2021-02-11-Introduccion-a-Python.ipynb"

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/prueba/2021-02-11-Introduccion-a-Python.ipynb
/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/2021-02-11-Introduccion-a-Python.ipynb

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
As we can see it is in its directory plus the copy I created in this notebook and saved it in the test folder.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
One very powerful thing about find is that we can use wildcards, for example if I want to find all the text files in my web folder

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!find ~/Documentos/web/ -name *.txt

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/2022-09-12 Introduccion a la terminal.txt
/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/prueba/lista.txt
/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/prueba/dot.txt
/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/prueba/dot2.txt
/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/prueba/secuential.txt
/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/prueba/subdirectorio/rootfile.txt
/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/prueba/subdirectorio/mitexto.txt
/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/prueba/file.txt
/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/wordpress_api_rest/page.txt

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
If we don't want it to be case sensitive we must use the -iname flag, for example if we search for all files containing the FILE tag, but using the -iname flag

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!find ~/Documentos/web/ -iname *FILE*

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/html_files
/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/prueba/subdirectorio/rootfile.txt
/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/prueba/file.txt

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
We see that all the results contain file and not FILE, i.e. it is not case sensitive.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
We can specify the file type with the -type flag. It only supports two types f for files and d for directories.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!find ~/Documentos/nerf -name image*

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
/home/wallabot/Documentos/nerf/instant-ngp/configs/image
/home/wallabot/Documentos/nerf/instant-ngp/dependencies/tiny-cuda-nn/benchmarks/image
/home/wallabot/Documentos/nerf/instant-ngp/dependencies/tiny-cuda-nn/dependencies/cutlass/media/images
/home/wallabot/Documentos/nerf/instant-ngp/dependencies/tiny-cuda-nn/dependencies/fmt/doc/bootstrap/mixins/image.less
/home/wallabot/Documentos/nerf/instant-ngp/dependencies/tiny-cuda-nn/data/images
/home/wallabot/Documentos/nerf/instant-ngp/dependencies/dlss/NVIDIAImageScaling/samples/media/images
/home/wallabot/Documentos/nerf/instant-ngp/data/nerf/fox/images
/home/wallabot/Documentos/nerf/instant-ngp/data/image

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!find ~/Documentos/nerf -name image* -type d

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
/home/wallabot/Documentos/nerf/instant-ngp/configs/image
/home/wallabot/Documentos/nerf/instant-ngp/dependencies/tiny-cuda-nn/benchmarks/image
/home/wallabot/Documentos/nerf/instant-ngp/dependencies/tiny-cuda-nn/dependencies/cutlass/media/images
/home/wallabot/Documentos/nerf/instant-ngp/dependencies/tiny-cuda-nn/data/images
/home/wallabot/Documentos/nerf/instant-ngp/dependencies/dlss/NVIDIAImageScaling/samples/media/images
/home/wallabot/Documentos/nerf/instant-ngp/data/nerf/fox/images
/home/wallabot/Documentos/nerf/instant-ngp/data/image

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!find ~/Documentos/nerf -name image* -type f

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
/home/wallabot/Documentos/nerf/instant-ngp/dependencies/tiny-cuda-nn/dependencies/fmt/doc/bootstrap/mixins/image.less

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
If we want to filter by file size we can use the -size flag, for example, if we want to search for all files larger than 200 MB

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!find ~/Documentos/ -type f -size +200M

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
/home/wallabot/Documentos/kaggle/hubmap/models/13_efficientnet-b7_final_model.pth
/home/wallabot/Documentos/kaggle/hubmap/models/12_efficientnet-b7_final_model.pth
/home/wallabot/Documentos/kaggle/hubmap/models/14_resnet152_final_model.pth
/home/wallabot/Documentos/kaggle/hubmap/models/14_resnet152_best_model.pth
/home/wallabot/Documentos/kaggle/hubmap/models/12_efficientnet-b7_early_stopping.pth
/home/wallabot/Documentos/kaggle/hubmap/models/efficientnet-b7-dcc49843.pth
/home/wallabot/Documentos/kaggle/hubmap/models/13_efficientnet-b7_early_stopping.pth

/home/wallabot/Documentos/kaggle/hubmap/models/12_efficientnet-b7_best_model.pth
/home/wallabot/Documentos/kaggle/hubmap/models/13_efficientnet-b7_best_model.pth

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
If we want to perform operations after the search we use the -exec flag.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
For example I will search for all folders with the name subdirectory.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!find ~/ -name subdirectorio -type d

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/prueba/subdirectorio

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
I can have them deleted with the -exec flag.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!find ~/ -name subdirectorio -type d -exec rm -r {} \;

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
rm: ¿borrar el fichero regular vacío '/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/prueba/subdirectorio/rootfile.txt'  protegido contra escritura? (s/n) s
find: ‘/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/prueba/subdirectorio’: No existe el archivo o el directorio

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!find ~/ -name subdirectorio -type d

								

							

						

					

				

			

				

					

						

							
Finally, if we use the ! character we will be indicating to find everything that does not match what we have specified.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!find ~/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/prueba ! -name *.txt

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/prueba
/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/prueba/index.html
/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/prueba/Abc
/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/prueba/datos1
/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/prueba/2021-02-11-Introduccion-a-Python.ipynb
/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/prueba/datos123

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
As we can see it has found everything that is not a .txt.

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
grep search command

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
grep is a very powerful search command, that is why we dedicate a section to it alone. The grep command uses regular expressions so if you want to learn about them I leave you a link to a post where I explain them.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
Let's start to see the power of this command, let's look for all the times the text MaximoFN appears inside the file 2021-02-11-Introduction-to-Python.ipynb.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
terminal("cd /home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/prueba")

								

							

						

					

				

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
terminal("grep MaximoFN 2021-02-11-Introduccion-a-Python.ipynb", max_lines_output=20)

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
        "a = 'MaximoFN'\n",
              "'MaximoFN'"
        "string = \"MaximoFN\"\n",
              "'MaximoFN'"
        "string = 'MaximoFN'\n",
              "'MaximoFN'"
            "Este es el blog de \"MaximoFN\"\n"
        "print(\"Este es el blog de \\\"MaximoFN\\\"\")"
            "Este es el blog de 'MaximoFN'\n"
        "print('Este es el blog de \\'MaximoFN\\'')"
            "Este es el blog de \\MaximoFN\\\n"
        "print('Este es el blog de \\\\MaximoFN\\\\')"
            "MaximoFN\n"
        "print('Este es el blog de \\nMaximoFN')"
            "Este es el blog de MaximoFN\n"
        "print('Esto no se imprimirá \\rEste es el blog de MaximoFN')"
            "Este es el blog de \tMaximoFN\n"
        "print('Este es el blog de \\tMaximoFN')"
            "Este es el blog deMaximoFN\n"
        "print('Este es el blog de \\bMaximoFN')"
	 ...
        "funcion2_del_modulo('MaximoFN')"
            "MaximoFN\n",
        "  print('MaximoFN')\n",
        "  variable = 'MaximoFN'\n",

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
However, if we do the same search for the text maximofn, we will find the text maximofn, maximofn and maximofn.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!grep maximofn 2021-02-11-Introduccion-a-Python.ipynb

								

							

						

					

				

			

				

					

						

							
No result appears, this is because grep is case sensitive i.e. it looks for the text as you have entered it, case sensitive. If we do not want this, we have to enter the -i flag.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
terminal("grep -i MaximoFN 2021-02-11-Introduccion-a-Python.ipynb", max_lines_output=20)

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
        "a = 'MaximoFN'\n",
              "'MaximoFN'"
        "string = \"MaximoFN\"\n",
              "'MaximoFN'"
        "string = 'MaximoFN'\n",
              "'MaximoFN'"
            "Este es el blog de \"MaximoFN\"\n"
        "print(\"Este es el blog de \\\"MaximoFN\\\"\")"
            "Este es el blog de 'MaximoFN'\n"
        "print('Este es el blog de \\'MaximoFN\\'')"
            "Este es el blog de \\MaximoFN\\\n"
        "print('Este es el blog de \\\\MaximoFN\\\\')"
            "MaximoFN\n"
        "print('Este es el blog de \\nMaximoFN')"
            "Este es el blog de MaximoFN\n"
        "print('Esto no se imprimirá \\rEste es el blog de MaximoFN')"
            "Este es el blog de \tMaximoFN\n"
        "print('Este es el blog de \\tMaximoFN')"
            "Este es el blog deMaximoFN\n"
        "print('Este es el blog de \\bMaximoFN')"
	 ...
        "funcion2_del_modulo('MaximoFN')"
            "MaximoFN\n",
        "  print('MaximoFN')\n",
        "  variable = 'MaximoFN'\n",

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
If what we want is to return the number of times it appears, we introduce the flag -c.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!grep -c MaximoFN 2021-02-11-Introduccion-a-Python.ipynb

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
105

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
If we do not care if it appears with upper or lower case, we can add the -i flag again, but it is not necessary to put it separately from the -c flag, they can be introduced together.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!grep -ci MaximoFN 2021-02-11-Introduccion-a-Python.ipynb

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
105

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
If now we want all the times in which no the word MaximumFN appears we instroduce the flag -v.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!grep -cv MaximoFN 2021-02-11-Introduccion-a-Python.ipynb

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
11573

							

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Network utilities

Information on network interfaces with ip.

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
Another way to get the information of our network interfaces is by using the ip command, adding a gives us the information of all the interfaces

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!ip a

       valid_lft forever preferred_lft forever

       valid_lft forever preferred_lft forever

Test communications with ping.

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
Another useful command is ping, which can be used to see if we have a connection to a certain IP. For example, googles IP is 142.250.200.78, so we ping it to see if it answers. The ping command in linux makes pings without stopping, so it never finishes until we stop it, so that this does not happen we add the flag -c` and the number of attempts.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!ping 142.250.200.132 -c 4

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
PING 142.250.200.132 (142.250.200.132) 56(84) bytes of data.
64 bytes from 142.250.200.132: icmp_seq=1 ttl=117 time=3.46 ms
64 bytes from 142.250.200.132: icmp_seq=2 ttl=117 time=3.77 ms
64 bytes from 142.250.200.132: icmp_seq=3 ttl=117 time=2.81 ms
64 bytes from 142.250.200.132: icmp_seq=4 ttl=117 time=2.86 ms
--- 142.250.200.132 ping statistics ---
4 packets transmitted, 4 received, 0% packet loss, time 3004ms
rtt min/avg/max/mdev = 2.812/3.227/3.773/0.405 ms

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
The same would have happened if we had done it directly on google.com.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!ping www.google.com -c 4

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
PING www.google.com (142.250.200.132) 56(84) bytes of data.
64 bytes from mad41s14-in-f4.1e100.net (142.250.200.132): icmp_seq=1 ttl=117 time=2.74 ms
64 bytes from mad41s14-in-f4.1e100.net (142.250.200.132): icmp_seq=2 ttl=117 time=3.96 ms
64 bytes from mad41s14-in-f4.1e100.net (142.250.200.132): icmp_seq=3 ttl=117 time=3.56 ms
64 bytes from mad41s14-in-f4.1e100.net (142.250.200.132): icmp_seq=4 ttl=117 time=2.87 ms
--- www.google.com ping statistics ---
4 packets transmitted, 4 received, 0% packet loss, time 3003ms
rtt min/avg/max/mdev = 2.741/3.283/3.962/0.499 ms

!curl https://www.google.com

Download files with wget

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
Another similar command is wget, however, unlike curl, wget downloads the file directly.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!wget https://www.google.com

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

Debugging the route with traceroute.

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
A very useful command is to see the route to a destination, for this we use traceroute, for example let's see all the places I have to go through to connect to google

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!traceroute www.google.com

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
traceroute to www.google.com (142.250.200.68), 64 hops max
  1   192.168.1.1  0,435ms  0,154ms  0,133ms 
  2   188.127.176.1  3,979ms  2,914ms  3,397ms 
  3   10.15.0.77  3,600ms  3,914ms  2,669ms 
  4   10.15.246.6  3,567ms  3,713ms  2,926ms 
  5   *  *  * 
  6   72.14.209.84  3,981ms  2,914ms  2,993ms 
  7   *  *  * 
  8   142.251.54.148  3,856ms  2,916ms  2,905ms 
  9   142.250.200.68  2,908ms  2,949ms  3,037ms 

							

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Debugging the route with mtr.

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
Another debugging tool is mtr, which is an enhanced version of traceroute. It provides information on each hop, such as response time, percentage of lost packets, etc.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!mtr -n maximofn.com

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

 3. (waiting for reply)

 8. (waiting for reply)
 9. 185.125.78.197                                                           2.9%   344    6.9   6.9   6.9   6.9   0.0

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
As you can see in jump 5, almost 50% of the packets are lost, so it would be useful for me to call my phone company and tell them to try to routing me somewhere else.

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Name of our machine with hostname.

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
If we want to know the name of our computer we can use hostname, which is useful if we want to connect to our machine from another one.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!hostname

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
wallabot

							

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Default gateway link information with route -n

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
If we want to know our default gateway we use the command route -n.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!route -n

IP information of a domain with nslookup.

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
If we want to know the IP of a domain we can know it with the command nslookup.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!nslookup google.com

This tells us that Google's IP v4 is 172.217.168.174 and its IP v6 is 2a00:1450:4003:803::200e.

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Information about our network with netstats.

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
The last utility command is netstats, this command gives us the status of our network, also with the flag -i it returns our network interfaces

!netstat -i

DNS queries with dig

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
With the command dig  we can make DNS queries, for example, let's make a query to google

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!dig google.com

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
; <<>> DiG 9.16.1-Ubuntu <<>> google.com

You can see

google.com. 283 IN A 142.250.184.14

						

					

				

			

	
	
	
	
			

				

					

						

							
So the query has given us the google IP

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
We can query a particular DNS server with dig @ .

!dig @1.1.1.1 google.com

We have made the same query, but we have made it to Cloudflare DNS

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Compressing files

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
Before compressing and decompressing let's see what we are going to compress, first we print our path and list the files

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!pwd; ls -l

!mkdir tocompress; cp * tocompress; ls -l

As we can see everything has been comped except the tocompress folder itself since we have not set the -r flag in the cp command. But what has happened is what we wanted

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Comrpimir con tar

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
The first command we are going to use to compress is tar to which we are going to add the -c flag for compress, -v for verbose, so that it will tell us what it is doing and the -f flag for file, and then the name we want the compressed file to have and the name of the file we want to compress.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!tar -cvf tocompress.tar tocompress

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
tocompress/
tocompress/lista.txt
tocompress/dot.txt
tocompress/google.html
tocompress/index.html
tocompress/Abc
tocompress/google2.html
tocompress/dot2.txt
tocompress/secuential.txt
tocompress/index.html.1
tocompress/file.txt
tocompress/datos1
tocompress/2021-02-11-Introduccion-a-Python.ipynb
tocompress/datos123

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!ls -l

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
total 676
-rw-rw-r-- 1 wallabot wallabot 285898 dic  6 00:28 2021-02-11-Introduccion-a-Python.ipynb
-rw-rw-r-- 1 wallabot wallabot      0 dic  6 00:56 Abc
-rw-rw-r-- 1 wallabot wallabot      0 dic  6 00:56 datos1

We see that you have created the file tocompress.tar.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
Now let's repeat the process, but adding the -z flag, this compresses in the gzip format, which is a more efficient compression algorithm.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!tar -cvzf tocompress.tar.gz tocompress

tocompress/datos123

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!ls -lh

Code:

					

						

							

								
!rm -r tocompress

								

							

						

					

				

			

				

					

						

							
Unzip

Compress with zip

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
Another command to compress the zip, to compress just add the flag -r (recursive), the name we want the compressed file to have and the file we want to compress.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!zip -r tocompress.zip tocompress

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
  adding: tocompress/ (stored 0%)

To decompress we use the command unzip followed by the name of the file we want to decompress. First we delete the folder tocompress.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!rm -r tocompress

!unzip tocompress.zip

Background and foreground processes

								
							

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Pause a process and take it to the background with CTRL+z.

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
When we run a process in the terminal, it may not stop running and we want to continue using the terminal, to solve this we can take the process to the background by pressing CTRL+z.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
For example, I can ping myself, without specifying the number of attempts, so this will be running until I stop the process by pressing CTRL+C.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!ping localhost

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
PING localhost (127.0.0.1) 56(84) bytes of data.
64 bytes from localhost (127.0.0.1): icmp_seq=1 ttl=64 time=0.025 ms
64 bytes from localhost (127.0.0.1): icmp_seq=2 ttl=64 time=0.027 ms
64 bytes from localhost (127.0.0.1): icmp_seq=3 ttl=64 time=0.024 ms
64 bytes from localhost (127.0.0.1): icmp_seq=4 ttl=64 time=0.024 ms
64 bytes from localhost (127.0.0.1): icmp_seq=5 ttl=64 time=0.027 ms
64 bytes from localhost (127.0.0.1): icmp_seq=6 ttl=64 time=0.036 ms
^C
--- localhost ping statistics ---
6 packets transmitted, 6 received, 0% packet loss, time 5127ms
rtt min/avg/max/mdev = 0.024/0.027/0.036/0.004 ms

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
However, if you want to stop the process for a moment in order to continue using the terminal, enter CTRL+Z.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!ping localhost

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
PING localhost (127.0.0.1) 56(84) bytes of data.
64 bytes from localhost (127.0.0.1): icmp_seq=1 ttl=64 time=0.028 ms
64 bytes from localhost (127.0.0.1): icmp_seq=2 ttl=64 time=0.020 ms
64 bytes from localhost (127.0.0.1): icmp_seq=3 ttl=64 time=0.017 ms
64 bytes from localhost (127.0.0.1): icmp_seq=4 ttl=64 time=0.025 ms
64 bytes from localhost (127.0.0.1): icmp_seq=5 ttl=64 time=0.021 ms
64 bytes from localhost (127.0.0.1): icmp_seq=6 ttl=64 time=0.055 ms
^Z
[1]+  Detenido                ping localhost

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
As indicated, the process has been stopped, it is not running while I have brought it into the background.

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
View background processes with jobs.

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
To see which processes are in the background we have two commands, on the one hand we can use jobs.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!jobs

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
jobs
[1]+  Detenido                ping localhost

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
This command gives us the process and the job number, this number will be the one we will use to take the process to the foreground.

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
View the background processes with ps.

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
Another command we can use is ps (processes)

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!ps

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
    PID TTY          TIME CMD
  16232 pts/3    00:00:00 bash
  17070 pts/3    00:00:00 ping
  18376 pts/3    00:00:00 ps

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
This command not only gives us the information of the processes running in background, but of all the processes running in the terminal

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
It is important to note that if we open a new terminal and use any of these commands it will not give us the ping information, since we execute it in another terminal

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!ps

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
    PID TTY          TIME CMD
  18993 pts/2    00:00:00 bash
  19290 pts/2    00:00:00 ps

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
As we can see jobs does not return anything because in this terminal there is no process in the background and ps only returns the bash information (the terminal itself) and the ps command launched.

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Take a process from the background to the foreground

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
To bring a process to the foreground you have to use the fg command, if you want to bring the last process that has been brought to the background just put fg, otherwise you have to indicate the job number.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
Let's go back to the processes we had in the background

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!jobs

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
[1]+  Detenido                ping localhost

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
We can take this process to the foreground just by typing fg or by indicating the job number with fg %1.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!fg %1

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
ping localhost
64 bytes from localhost (127.0.0.1): icmp_seq=7 ttl=64 time=0.032 ms
64 bytes from localhost (127.0.0.1): icmp_seq=8 ttl=64 time=0.036 ms
64 bytes from localhost (127.0.0.1): icmp_seq=9 ttl=64 time=0.045 ms
64 bytes from localhost (127.0.0.1): icmp_seq=10 ttl=64 time=0.035 ms
64 bytes from localhost (127.0.0.1): icmp_seq=11 ttl=64 time=0.031 ms

							

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Running a process directly in the background

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
Let's relaunch the firefox browser now by typing firefox in the terminal and press CTRL+Z to bring it to the background

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!firefox

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
[GFX1-]: glxtest: VA-API test failed: failed to initialise VAAPI connection.
[2022-11-29T06:16:17Z ERROR glean_core::metrics::ping] Invalid reason code startup for ping background-update
^Z
[1]+  Detenido                firefox

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
As we can see, it says that the process has been stopped, so if we now want to browse firefox we cannot, since it is stopped.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
To be able to launch firefox and have it in background so that it does not block the terminal, you have to write a & at the end of the command

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!firefox &

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
[1] 23663
$ [GFX1-]: glxtest: VA-API test failed: failed to initialise VAAPI connection.
[2022-11-29T06:19:40Z ERROR glean_core::metrics::ping] Invalid reason code startup for ping background-update
$

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
Now we launch firefox, it tells us its job number and it stays in the background running

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
In fact, if we now look at the processes, we can see the status of firefox is Running.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!jobs

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
[1]+  Ejecutando              firefox &

							

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Terminating a background process

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
Since we have firefox running in background, if we want it to terminate we have to use kill and the job number of the process. Let's use jobs to see the job number of firefox

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!jobs

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
[1]+  Ejecutando              firefox &

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
Its job number is 1, so we set that number to end the process.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!kill %1

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
It doesn't answer anything, but if we use jobs again to see the processes in background

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!jobs

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
[1]+  Terminado               firefox

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
We see that it tells us that firefox has finished, if we run jobs again, nothing will appear.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!jobs

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								

							

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Terminal-independent background processing

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
So far we have seen how to run terminal-dependent background processes, that is, if we send a process to the background and close the terminal, it will send an end message to all the processes that are in its background and they will terminate.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
But there are times when we want the process to stay in background, to be able to close the terminal, open another one and recover that process, for that we will use tmux, which is a terminal multiplexer.

						

					

				

			

	
			

				

					

						

							
It is possible that it comes installed, so to install it you have to enter the following

						

					

				

			

	
			

				

					

						

							
sudo apt install tmux

						

					

				

			

	
	
			

				

					

						

							
Once installed we can create a new tmux session using tmux new -s .

						

					

				

			

	
			

				

					

						

							
tmux new -s session1

						

					

				

			

	
	
	
			

				

					

						

							
This will open a new terminal, which is actually a tmux session.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
Within it we can launch a process, for example a ping to ourselves.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!ping localhost

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
PING localhost (127.0.0.1) 56(84) bytes of data.
64 bytes from localhost (127.0.0.1): icmp_seq=1 ttl=64 time=0.036 ms
64 bytes from localhost (127.0.0.1): icmp_seq=2 ttl=64 time=0.019 ms
64 bytes from localhost (127.0.0.1): icmp_seq=3 ttl=64 time=0.031 ms

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
We can close that terminal

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
If we open a new one, we can see all open tmux sessions with the command tmux ls.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!tmux ls

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
session1: 1 windows (created Tue Nov 29 08:15:22 2022)

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
And we can enter a session using the tmux command, followed by a (attach), -t (tag) and the session name

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!tmux a -t session1

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
64 bytes from localhost (127.0.0.1): icmp_seq=146 ttl=64 time=0.025 ms
64 bytes from localhost (127.0.0.1): icmp_seq=147 ttl=64 time=0.022 ms
64 bytes from localhost (127.0.0.1): icmp_seq=148 ttl=64 time=0.026 ms
64 bytes from localhost (127.0.0.1): icmp_seq=149 ttl=64 time=0.013 ms
64 bytes from localhost (127.0.0.1): icmp_seq=150 ttl=64 time=0.027 ms
64 bytes from localhost (127.0.0.1): icmp_seq=151 ttl=64 time=0.019 ms

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
As we can see, we have recovered the ping of the past.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
When we are inside a session we can exit (kill) it by typing CTRL+D or by typing exit.

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Process management

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
To manage the processes of our system we have several options

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
ps process handler

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
As we have seen before ps gives us the processes that are running in our terminal. For example we run a process in the background and we see the processes with ps.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!firefox &

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
[1] 50555

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
We now look at the processes

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!ps

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
    PID TTY          TIME CMD
  36660 pts/3    00:00:00 bash
  50555 pts/3    00:00:02 firefox
  50613 pts/3    00:00:00 Socket Process
  50635 pts/3    00:00:00 Privileged Cont
  50683 pts/3    00:00:00 WebExtensions
  50741 pts/3    00:00:00 Web Content
  50743 pts/3    00:00:00 Web Content
  50748 pts/3    00:00:00 Web Content
  50840 pts/3    00:00:00 ps

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
Once we know its PID we can kill the process

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!kill 50555

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
We now look at the processes

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!ps

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
    PID TTY          TIME CMD
  36660 pts/3    00:00:00 bash
  51132 pts/3    00:00:00 ps

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
But as we have said, the bad thing about ps is that it only shows the processes of your terminal.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
To show us all the processes of the system we have to add aux.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
terminal("ps aux", max_lines_output=10)

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

Now, if what I want is to search only the processes that my user is running, I can do it by creating a pipe and searching with grep.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!ps aux | grep wallabot

Top Process Handler

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
With top we can see all the processes of the operating system. It will show us the information with less, so as explained before, when you want to stop top, entering q will stop

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!top

The good thing about top is that it gives us many more utilities, if we press h it will show us help

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!h

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

  10135 wallabot  20   0  826220  58164  41648 S   1,0   0,2   0:14.56 gnome-t+ 
   6635 wallabot  20   0   33,0g 647936 436252 S   0,3   2,0   4:01.80 chrome

   1114 wallabot  20   0  169792   3636     12 S   0,0   0,0   0:00.00 (sd-pam) 
   1121 wallabot  39  19  591436  37256  16676 S   0,0   0,1   0:03.19 tracker+ 
   1124 wallabot  20   0  390740   8688   7416 S   0,0   0,0   0:00.45 gnome-k+ 
   1128 wallabot  20   0  166804   6596   5956 S   0,0   0,0   0:00.00 gdm-x-s+ 
   1134 wallabot  20   0    9152   6144   3796 S   0,0   0,0   0:01.81 dbus-da+

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
Another way of looking at this would be to be able to filter by user would be to make a pipeline and using grep

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!top | grep wallabot

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
   1440 wallabot  20   0 4684708 505432 118024 S   6,7   1,5   2:05.92 gnome-s+ 
  25326 wallabot  20   0 1133,0g 527912 123732 S   1,7   1,6   1:31.73 chrome

  34284 wallabot  20   0   14876   4376   3308 R   0,3   0,0   0:00.02 top

							

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
htop process handler

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
Similar to top but more powerful

						

					

				

			

	
			

				

					

						

							
You probably don't have it installed, so to install it enter the command

						

					

				

			

	
			

				

					

						

							
sudo apt install htop

						

					

				

			

	
	
	
			

				

					

						

							
o

						

					

				

			

	
			

				

					

						

							
sudo snap install htop

						

					

				

			

	
	
			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Process manager glances

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
Similar to top but more powerful

						

					

				

			

	
			

				

					

						

							
You probably don't have it installed, so to install it enter the command

						

					

				

			

	
			

				

					

						

							
sudo apt install glances

						

					

				

			

	
	
			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
RAM management

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
If we only want to obtain memory information we can use the command free.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!free

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
              total       usado       libre  compartido búfer/caché  disponible
Memoria:    32774516     6563544    20091804      276296     6119168    25479600
Swap:       2097148           0     2097148

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
But as this information is not very easy to digest, we add the flag -h (human), to make it easier to read.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!free -h

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
              total       usado       libre  compartido búfer/caché  disponible
Memoria:        31Gi       6,3Gi        19Gi       270Mi       5,8Gi        24Gi
Swap:         2,0Gi          0B       2,0Gi

							

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Hard disk management

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
To obtain information from the hard disk we use the du command, if we only enter this command via terminal it will give us the information of all the folders of our machine, so, in order not to obtain too much information, it is necessary to enter a path we want to scan

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!du ~/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
8	/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/__pycache__
1648	/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/notebooks_translated
4288	/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/html_files
336	/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/prueba/tocompress
1132	/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/prueba
16	/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/introduccion_python/__pycache__
28	/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/introduccion_python
11232	/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
As before, we added the -h (human) flag to make it easier to read

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!du ~/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/ -h

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
8,0K	/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/__pycache__
1,7M	/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/notebooks_translated
4,2M	/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/html_files
336K	/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/prueba/tocompress
1,2M	/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/prueba
16K	/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/introduccion_python/__pycache__
28K	/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/introduccion_python
11M	/home/wallabot/Documentos/web/portafolio/posts/

							

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Interface management

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
In Ubuntu, by default we boot to a graphical interface, but we can open other interfaces, which will not be graphical, by entering CTRL+ALT+F where the number can go from 1 to 6. Only 2 will have the graphical interface and 1 will be the login

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
When handling several interfaces we may not know which one we are on, so entering the tty command will tell us which one we are on.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!tty

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
/dev/pts/0

							

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Package handling

								
							

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
PPA repositories (Personal Package Archives)

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
In Linux, package management is done through repositories. This is a list of addresses where the binaries of our programs are located. So when we want to update or install our programs (later we will explain how), what the operating system will do is to see the list of these repositories and go to the indicated addresses to look for the binaries.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
This list of repositories is located in /etc/apt/sources.list and inside the /etc/apt/sources.list.d folder. Let's take a look at this list

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
terminal("cat /etc/apt/sources.list", max_lines_output=10)

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
# deb cdrom:[Ubuntu 20.04.2.0 LTS _Focal Fossa_ - Release amd64 (20210209.1)]/ focal main restricted
# See http://help.ubuntu.com/community/UpgradeNotes for how to upgrade to
# newer versions of the distribution.
deb http://es.archive.ubuntu.com/ubuntu/ focal main restricted
# deb-src http://es.archive.ubuntu.com/ubuntu/ focal main restricted
## Major bug fix updates produced after the final release of the
## distribution.
deb http://es.archive.ubuntu.com/ubuntu/ focal-updates main restricted
	 ...
deb https://developer.download.nvidia.com/compute/cuda/repos/ubuntu2004/x86_64/ /
# deb-src https://developer.download.nvidia.com/compute/cuda/repos/ubuntu2004/x86_64/ /
deb https://apt.kitware.com/ubuntu/ focal main
# deb-src https://apt.kitware.com/ubuntu/ focal main

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
The first lines that include the word cd-rom are references to the installation cd, they always come with the words deb cdrom: even if it was installed via network or usb.

						

					

				

			

	
			

				

					

						

							
From here, several lines starting with deb or deb-src begin to appear. In deb are the binaries and in deb-src is the source code.

						

					

				

			

	
			

				

					

						

							
Every valid repository address has one of these formats:

						

					

				

			

	
			

				

					

						

							

								
    
									
  • deb http://direccion_del_servidor/nombre_carpeta version_name (main or universe or multiverse or main restricted, etc.)
    • 
									
  • deb-src http://direccion_del_servidor/nombre_carpeta version_name (main or universe or multiverse or main restricted, etc.)
    • 
								

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						


						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
The six types of Ubuntu repositories are :

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							

								
    
									
  1. Main
    2. 
									
  2. Universe
    3. 
								

							

						

					

				

			

	
			

				

					

						

							
Main is Ubuntu's main repository. The Main repository is enabled by default and contains only free and open source software or FOSS (Free and Open Source Software). All software provided by Main can be distributed freely and without restrictions.

						

					

				

			

	
			

				

					

						


						

					

				

			

	
			

				

					

						

							
Like Main, Universe also offers FOSS. The difference is that in this repository it is not Ubuntu who guarantees regular security updates, but the community who is in charge of its support. It is enabled by default, but not always. Some operating systems have it disabled by default and we may have to enable it if we are running a Live Session. If we do not have it added, we can do it with this command:

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
    

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
sudo add-apt-repository universe

						

					

				

			

	
	
	
			

				

					

						

							
What do we find in "Universe"? I would say most of the worthwhile software, among which we have VLC or OpenShot.

						

					

				

			

	
			

				

					

						

							

								
    
									
  1. Multiverse
    2. 
									
  2. Restricted
    3. 
									
  3. Partner
    4. 
									
  4. Third-party Ubuntu repositories
    5. 
								

							

						

					

				

			

	
			

				

					

						

							
From here come the Ubuntu repositories with less freedom. Multiverse contains software that is no longer FOSS and Ubuntu cannot activate this repository by default due to legal and licensing issues. On the other hand, it also cannot provide patches and updates. With this in mind, we have to consider whether to add it or not, which we can do with this command:

						

					

				

			

	
			

				

					

						

							
sudo add-apt-repository multiverse

						

					

				

			

	
	
	
			

				

					

						


						

					

				

			

	
			

				

					

						

							
In the Ubuntu repositories we can find free and open source software, but this is not possible when it comes to something related to hardware. In the Restricted repositories we will find drivers, such as those for graphics cards, touch panels or network cards.

						

					

				

			

	
			

				

					

						

							
sudo add-apt-repository restricted

						

					

				

			

	
	
	
			

				

					

						


						

					

				

			

	
			

				

					

						

							
This repository contains proprietary software compiled by Ubuntu from its partners.

						

					

				

			

	
			

				

					

						


						

					

				

			

	
			

				

					

						

							
Finally, there are the third-party repositories. Ubuntu tries to always offer the best user experience and that is one of the reasons why it rejects certain software. There are also developers who prefer to have full control over what they offer and for that reason they create their own repositories.

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Add repositories

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
If we are in Ubuntu we can add a repository using the command add-apt-repository . But as in other distributions other than Ubuntu we do not have this command

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Update repositories

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
Using the apt update command we can update the latest versions of the packages in our repository.

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Upgrade packages

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
By means of the apt upgrade command we will be able to update the programs that we have installed and whose repository has been updated before.

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Update the kernel

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
If the apt dist-upgrade command is used, kernel packages will also be upgraded.

						

					

				

			

	
			

				

					

						

							
 > Caution: Upgrading kernel packages may cause some packages to break.

						

					

				

			

	
			

				

					

						

							
 > Reminder: When updating kernel packages, it is necessary to restart the computer for them to take effect.

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Packet search

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
With the command apt search  we can find packages

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
terminal("apt search vlc", max_lines_output=10)

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
Ordenando...
Buscar en todo el texto...
anacrolix-dms/focal 1.1.0-1 amd64
  Go UPnP DLNA Digital Media Server with basic video transcoding
cubemap/focal 1.4.3-1build1 amd64
  scalable video reflector, designed to be used with VLC
dvblast/focal 3.4-1 amd64
  Simple and powerful dvb-streaming application
	 ...
x264/focal 2:0.155.2917+git0a84d98-2 amd64
  video encoder for the H.264/MPEG-4 AVC standard

							

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
List of installed packages

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
To see what packages we have installed we can use dpkg -l, this will give us a list of all the packages we have installed on our computer

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
terminal("dpkg -l", max_lines_output=10)

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
Deseado=desconocido(U)/Instalar/eliminaR/Purgar/retener(H)

||/ Nombre                                     Versión                               Arquitectura Descripción
+++-==========================================-=====================================-============-===========================================================================================================================================================================================================================================================================================================================================================================================================================================
ii  accountsservice                            0.6.55-0ubuntu12~20.04.5              amd64        query and manipulate user account information

	 ...

ii  zlib1g-dev:amd64                           1:1.2.11.dfsg-2ubuntu1.5              amd64        compression library - development

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
If we want to search if we have a package installed, we can use the previous command and create a pipe and search for the package name with grep.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
terminal("dpkg -l | grep vlc")

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
Deseado=desconocido(U)/Instalar/eliminaR/Purgar/retener(H)

|/ Err?=(ninguno)/requiere-Reinst (Estado,Err: mayúsc.=malo)
||/ Nombre         Versión      Arquitectura Descripción
+++-==============-============-============-=================================
ii  grep           3.4-1        amd64        GNU grep, egrep and fgrep
ii  vlc            3.0.9.2-1    amd64        multimedia player and streamer

							

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Install downloaded packages and not from repositories

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
Sometimes, when you want to install a program, what they do is that they give you a .deb file, so to install it we use the command dpkg -i .

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
User administrator

								
							

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Active user information with id.

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
By using the id command I can see which user I am

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!id

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
uid=1000(wallabot) gid=1000(wallabot) grupos=1000(wallabot),4(adm),24(cdrom),27(sudo),30(dip),46(plugdev),120(lpadmin),131(lxd),132(sambashare),998(docker)

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
With this command I can see my id, the group id gid and the groups I belong to. On debian based distributions users are given an id starting at 1000, while the root user is assigned id 0.

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Active user information with whoami

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
Another command to know which user I am is whoami.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!whoami

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
wallabot

							

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
File with information about all users

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
The user information is in the file /etc/passwd.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
terminal("cat /etc/passwd", max_lines_output=10)

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
root:x:0:0:root:/root:/bin/bash
daemon:x:1:1:daemon:/usr/sbin:/usr/sbin/nologin
bin:x:2:2:bin:/bin:/usr/sbin/nologin
sys:x:3:3:sys:/dev:/usr/sbin/nologin
sync:x:4:65534:sync:/bin:/bin/sync
games:x:5:60:games:/usr/games:/usr/sbin/nologin
man:x:6:12:man:/var/cache/man:/usr/sbin/nologin
lp:x:7:7:lp:/var/spool/lpd:/usr/sbin/nologin
mail:x:8:8:mail:/var/mail:/usr/sbin/nologin
news:x:9:9:news:/var/spool/news:/usr/sbin/nologin
	 ...
sshd:x:126:65534::/run/sshd:/usr/sbin/nologin
nvidia-persistenced:x:127:135:NVIDIA Persistence Daemon,,,:/nonexistent:/usr/sbin/nologin
fwupd-refresh:x:128:136:fwupd-refresh user,,,:/run/systemd:/usr/sbin/nologin
glances:x:129:137::/var/lib/glances:/usr/sbin/nologin

							

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Change a user's password

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
If I want to change the password of a user I have to use the command passwd  , if the user is not specified, the whoami command will be taken.

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Create users with useradd

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
To create a new user use the command useradd , we will create a new user

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!sudo useradd usertest1

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
Let's see if it is in the file with all users

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!cat /etc/passwd | grep usertest

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
usertest1:x:1001:1001::/home/usertest1:/bin/sh

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
As we can see, the user has been created with the id 1001, the next to the one my user wallabot had, which was the last one.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
However, when creating this user it has not asked us to assign a password. In addition, if we look at what is inside home

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!ls /home

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
wallabot

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
Only the wallabot user folder is here, but not the test1 user folder.

						

					

				

			

			
			

				

					

						

							

								
									
Create users with adduser.

								
							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
So let's look at another command to create users that does ask for a password and does create a folder in home. This command is adduser.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!sudo adduser usertest2

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
[sudo] contraseña para wallabot: 
Añadiendo el usuario `usertest2' ...
Añadiendo el nuevo grupo `usertest2' (1002) ...
Añadiendo el nuevo usuario `usertest2' (1002) con grupo `usertest2' ...
Creando el directorio personal `/home/usertest2' ...
Copiando los ficheros desde `/etc/skel' ...
Nueva contraseña: 
Vuelva a escribir la nueva contraseña:

Cambiando la información de usuario para usertest2
Introduzca el nuevo valor, o presione INTRO para el predeterminado
	Nombre completo []: 
	Número de habitación []: 
	Teléfono del trabajo []: 
	Teléfono de casa []: 
	Otro []: 
¿Es correcta la información? [S/n] s

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
If we look in the file with all the users

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!cat /etc/passwd | grep usertest

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
usertest1:x:1001:1001::/home/usertest1:/bin/sh

We see that the user usertest2 has been created.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!ls /home

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
usertest2  wallabot

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
And we see that a folder has been created for it in home.

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
To delete a user you must use the command userdel .

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!sudo userdel usertest1

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!sudo userdel usertest2

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								

							

						

					

				

			

			

				

					

						

							
Let's see if they are in the file with all users

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!cat /etc/passwd | grep usertest

								

							

						

					

				

			

				

					

						

							
We see that nothing appears anymore, in fact we see the end of the file

						

					

				

			

			

				

					
Code:

					

						

							

								
!tail /etc/passwd

								

							

						

					

				

				

					

						

							
Output:

							

								
geoclue:x:122:127::/var/lib/geoclue:/usr/sbin/nologin
